<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Marco Polo</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the trader and explorer. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_(disambiguation)" title="Marco Polo (disambiguation)">Marco Polo (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Venetian merchant who explored Asia</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Marco Polo</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-marco_polo.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/En-us-marco_polo.ogg" title="En-us-marco polo.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>, <small>Venetian: </small><span class="IPA" lang="vec-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Venetian" title="Help:IPA/Venetian">[ˈmaɾko ˈpolo]</a></span>, <small>Italian: </small><span class="IPA" lang="it-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[ˈmarko ˈpɔːlo]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:MarcoPolo.flac" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/MarcoPolo.flac" title="MarcoPolo.flac">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; c. 1254 – 8 January 1324)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340–42_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340–42-1">[1]</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venetian</a> merchant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> explorer and writer who travelled through Asia along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> between 1271 and 1295. His travels are recorded in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">The Travels of Marco Polo</a></i> (also known as <i>Book of the Marvels of the World </i> and <i>Il Milione</i>, c. 1300), a book that described to Europeans the then mysterious culture and inner workings of the Eastern world, including the wealth and great size of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> and China in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a>, giving their first comprehensive look into <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and other Asian cities and countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, Marco learned the mercantile trade from his father and his uncle, <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_and_Maffeo_Polo" title="Niccolò and Maffeo Polo">Niccolò and Maffeo</a>, who travelled through Asia and met <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>. In 1269, they returned to Venice to meet Marco for the first time. The three of them embarked on an epic journey to Asia, exploring many places along the Silk Road until they reached <a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a> (China). They were received by the royal court of Kublai Khan, who was impressed by Marco's intelligence and humility. Marco was appointed to serve as Khan's foreign emissary, and he was sent on many <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_mission" title="Diplomatic mission">diplomatic missions</a> throughout the empire and Southeast Asia, such as in present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rongguang_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rongguang-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleinhenz_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kleinhenz-6">[6]</a></sup> As part of this appointment, Marco also travelled extensively inside China, living in the emperor's lands for 17 years and seeing many things that had previously been unknown to Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldAtlas_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-WorldAtlas-7">[7]</a></sup> Around 1291, the Polos also offered to accompany the Mongol princess <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6k%C3%B6chin" title="Kököchin">Kököchin</a> to Persia; they arrived around 1293. After leaving the princess, they travelled overland to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and then to <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, returning home after 24 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldAtlas_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-WorldAtlas-7">[7]</a></sup> At this time, Venice was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venetian%E2%80%93Genoese_Wars" title="Venetian–Genoese Wars">at war with</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoa</a>; Marco was captured and imprisoned by the Genoans after joining the war effort and dictated his stories to <a href="/wiki/Rustichello_da_Pisa" title="Rustichello da Pisa">Rustichello da Pisa</a>, a cellmate. He was released in 1299, became a wealthy <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">merchant</a>, married, and had three children. He died in 1324 and was buried in the church of <a href="/wiki/San_Lorenzo,_Venice" title="San Lorenzo, Venice">San Lorenzo in Venice</a>.
</p><p>Though he was not the first European to reach China  (see <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">Europeans in Medieval China</a>), Marco Polo was the first to leave a detailed chronicle of his experience. This account of the Orient provided the Europeans with a clear picture of the East's geography and ethnic customs, and was the first Western record of porcelain, coal, gunpowder, paper money, and some Asian plants and exotic animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-8">[8]</a></sup> His travel book inspired <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landström_1967_27_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landström_1967_27-9">[9]</a></sup> and many other travellers. There is substantial literature based on Polo's writings; he also influenced European <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>, leading to the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_map" title="Fra Mauro map">Fra Mauro map</a>.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birthplace_and_family_origin">Birthplace and family origin</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Polo_portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="311" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Marco_Polo_portrait.jpg/170px-Marco_Polo_portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Marco_Polo_portrait.jpg/255px-Marco_Polo_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/Marco_Polo_portrait.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Polo_portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>16th-century portrait of Marco Polo</div></div></div>
<p>Marco Polo was born in 1254 in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, the capital of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venetian_Republic" title="Venetian Republic">Venetian Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HKJ_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-HKJ-11">[11]</a></sup> His father, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Polo" title="Niccolò Polo">Niccolò Polo</a>, had his household in Venice and left Marco's pregnant mother in order to travel to Asia with his brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maffeo_Polo" title="Maffeo Polo">Maffeo Polo</a>. Their return to Italy in order to "go to Venice and visit their household" is described in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">The Travels of Marco Polo</a></i> as follows: "...they departed from <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">Acre</a> and went to <a href="/wiki/Euboea" title="Euboea">Negropont</a>, and from Negropont they continued their voyage to Venice. On their arrival there, Messer Nicolas found that his wife was dead and that she had left behind her a son of fifteen years of age, whose name was Marco".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>His first known ancestor was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_uncle" title="Great uncle">great uncle</a>, Marco Polo (the older) from Venice, who lent some money and commanded a ship in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>. Andrea, Marco's grandfather, lived in Venice in "<a href="/wiki/Contrada" title="Contrada">contrada</a> <a href="/wiki/San_Felice,_Venice" title="San Felice, Venice">San Felice"</a>, he had three sons: Marco "the older", <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_and_Maffeo_Polo" title="Niccolò and Maffeo Polo">Maffeo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_and_Maffeo_Polo" title="Niccolò and Maffeo Polo">Niccolò</a> (Marco's father).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergreen25_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bergreen25-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Some old Venetian historical sources considered Polo's ancestors to be of far <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatian</a> origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PeklicPolo_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-PeklicPolo-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuljiz-Šostik20155–16_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuljiz-Šostik20155–16-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nickname_Milione">Nickname <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Milione</i></span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VENEZIA_MILION.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4320" data-file-width="3240" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/VENEZIA_MILION.JPG/170px-VENEZIA_MILION.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/VENEZIA_MILION.JPG/255px-VENEZIA_MILION.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/VENEZIA_MILION.JPG/340px-VENEZIA_MILION.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VENEZIA_MILION.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Corte Seconda del Milion</i></span>, Venice is named after the nickname of Polo, <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Il Milione</i></span></div></div></div>
<p>Marco Polo is most often mentioned in the archives of the Republic of Venice as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Marco Paulo de confinio Sancti Iohannis Grisostomi</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unive.it_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-unive.it-18">[18]</a></sup> which means Marco Polo of the <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">contrada</i></span> of <a href="/wiki/San_Giovanni_Grisostomo,_Venice" title="San Giovanni Grisostomo, Venice">St John Chrysostom Church</a>.
</p><p>However, he was also nicknamed <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Milione</i></span> during his lifetime (which in Italian literally means 'Million'). In fact, the Italian title of his book was <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Il libro di Marco Polo detto il Milione</i></span>, which means "The Book of Marco Polo, nicknamed '<span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Milione</i></span><span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>". According to the 15th-century humanist <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Ramusio" title="Giovanni Battista Ramusio">Giovanni Battista Ramusio</a>, his fellow citizens awarded him this nickname when he came back to Venice because he kept on saying that Kublai Khan's wealth was counted in millions. More precisely, he was nicknamed <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Messer Marco Milioni</i></span> (Mr Marco Millions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, since also his father Niccolò was nicknamed <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Milione</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> 19th-century philologist Luigi Foscolo Benedetto was persuaded that <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Milione</i></span> was a shortened version of <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Emilione</i></span>, and that this nickname was used to distinguish Niccolò's and Marco's branch from other Polo families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allulli_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-allulli-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_Asian_travel">Early life and Asian travel</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_and_Maffeo_Polo" title="Niccolò and Maffeo Polo">Niccolò and Maffeo Polo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">Europeans in Medieval China</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Polo_Mosaic_from_Palazzo_Tursi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1286" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Marco_Polo_Mosaic_from_Palazzo_Tursi.jpg/170px-Marco_Polo_Mosaic_from_Palazzo_Tursi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Marco_Polo_Mosaic_from_Palazzo_Tursi.jpg/255px-Marco_Polo_Mosaic_from_Palazzo_Tursi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Marco_Polo_Mosaic_from_Palazzo_Tursi.jpg/340px-Marco_Polo_Mosaic_from_Palazzo_Tursi.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Polo_Mosaic_from_Palazzo_Tursi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mosaic of Marco Polo displayed in the Palazzo Doria-Tursi, <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, Italy</div></div></div>
<p>In 1168, his great-uncle, Marco Polo, borrowed money and commanded a ship in Constantinople.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen200725_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen200725-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rugoff_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rugoff-24">[24]</a></sup> His grandfather, Andrea Polo of the parish of San Felice, had three sons, Maffeo, yet another Marco, and the traveller's father Niccolò.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen200725_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen200725-23">[23]</a></sup> This genealogy, described by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Ramusio" title="Giovanni Battista Ramusio">Ramusio</a>, is not universally accepted as there is no additional evidence to support it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENoule&amp;Pelliot193815–16_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENoule&amp;Pelliot193815–16-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljica_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poljica-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>His father, <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_and_Maffeo_Polo" title="Niccolò and Maffeo Polo">Niccolò Polo</a>, a merchant, traded with the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, becoming wealthy and achieving great prestige.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica571_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica571-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup> Niccolò and his brother Maffeo set off on a trading voyage before Marco's birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Italiani_nel_sistema_solare_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Italiani_nel_sistema_solare-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1260, Niccolò and Maffeo, while residing in Constantinople, then the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Empire" title="Latin Empire">Latin Empire</a>, foresaw a political change; they liquidated their assets into jewels and moved away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica571_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica571-27">[27]</a></sup> According to <i>The Travels of Marco Polo</i>, they passed through much of Asia, and met with <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, a Mongol ruler and founder of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Their decision to leave Constantinople proved timely. In 1261 <a href="/wiki/Michael_VIII_Palaiologos" title="Michael VIII Palaiologos">Michael VIII Palaiologos</a>, the ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Nicaea" title="Empire of Nicaea">Empire of Nicaea</a>, took Constantinople, promptly burned the Venetian quarter and re-established the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. Captured Venetian citizens were blinded,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> while many of those who managed to escape perished aboard overloaded refugee ships fleeing to other Venetian colonies in the Aegean Sea.
</p><p>Almost nothing is known about the childhood of Marco Polo until he was fifteen years old, except that he probably spent part of his childhood in Venice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen200736_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen200736-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuljiz-Šostik201524_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuljiz-Šostik201524-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rugoff_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rugoff-24">[24]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Marco Polo's mother died, and an aunt and uncle raised him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup> He received a good education, learning mercantile subjects including foreign currency, appraising, and the handling of cargo ships;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup> he learned little or no <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica571_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica571-27">[27]</a></sup> His father later married Floradise Polo (née Trevisan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljica_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Poljica-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1269, Niccolò and Maffeo returned to their families in Venice, meeting young Marco for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen200736_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen200736-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1271, during the rule of <a href="/wiki/Doge" title="Doge">Doge</a> <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Tiepolo" title="Lorenzo Tiepolo">Lorenzo Tiepolo</a>, Marco Polo (at seventeen years of age), his father, and his uncle set off for Asia on the series of adventures that Marco later documented in his book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen200737_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen200737-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>They sailed to <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">Acre</a> and later rode on their camels to the Persian port <a href="/wiki/Hormuz,_Iran" title="Hormuz, Iran">Hormuz</a>. During the first stages of the journey, they stayed for a few months in Acre and were able to speak with Archdeacon <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_X" title="Pope Gregory X">Tedaldo Visconti of Piacenza</a>. The Polo family, on that occasion, had expressed their regret at the long lack of a pope, because on their previous trip to China they had received a letter from Kublai Khan to the Pope, and had thus had to leave for China disappointed. During the trip, however, they received news that after 33 months of vacation, finally, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conclave" title="Conclave">Conclave</a> had elected the new Pope and that he was exactly the archdeacon of Acre. The three of them hurried to return to the Holy Land, where the new Pope entrusted them with letters for the "Great Khan", inviting him to send his emissaries to Rome. To give more weight to this mission he sent with the Polos, as his legates, two <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> fathers, Guglielmo of Tripoli and Nicola of Piacenza.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>They continued overland until they arrived at <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>'s place in <a href="/wiki/Shangdu" title="Shangdu">Shangdu</a>, China (then known as <a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a>). By this time, Marco was 21 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-totallyhistory_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-totallyhistory-36">[36]</a></sup> Impressed by Marco's intelligence and humility, Khan appointed him to serve as his foreign emissary to <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>. He was sent on many diplomatic missions throughout his empire and in Southeast Asia, (such as in present-day <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rongguang_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rongguang-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleinhenz_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kleinhenz-6">[6]</a></sup> but also entertained the Khan with stories and observations about the lands he saw. As part of this appointment, Marco travelled extensively inside China, living in the emperor's lands for 17 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldAtlas_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-WorldAtlas-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kublai initially refused several times to let the Polos return to Europe, as he appreciated their company and they became useful to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> However, around 1291, he finally granted permission, entrusting the Polos with his last duty: accompany the Mongol princess <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6k%C3%B6chin" title="Kököchin">Kököchin</a>, who was to become the consort of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arghun_Khan" title="Arghun Khan">Arghun Khan</a>, in Persia (see <i><a href="#Narrative">Narrative</a></i> section).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-totallyhistory_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-totallyhistory-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Igor_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Igor-38">[38]</a></sup> After leaving the princess, the Polos travelled overland to Constantinople. They later decided to return to their home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-totallyhistory_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-totallyhistory-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>They returned to Venice in 1295, after 24 years, with many riches and treasures. They had travelled almost 15,000 miles (24,000 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genoese_captivity_and_later_life">Genoese captivity and later life</span></h3>
<p>Marco Polo returned to Venice in 1295 with his fortune converted into <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gemstones</a>. At this time, Venice was at war with the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Republic of Genoa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donald_M._Nicol219_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donald_M._Nicol219-39">[39]</a></sup> Polo armed a galley equipped with a <a href="/wiki/Trebuchet" title="Trebuchet">trebuchet</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pierriere_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pierriere-40">[40]</a></sup> to join the war. He was probably caught by Genoans in a skirmish in 1296, off the <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a> coast between <a href="/wiki/Adana" title="Adana">Adana</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_%C4%B0skenderun" title="Gulf of İskenderun">Gulf of Alexandretta</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> (and not during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Curzola" title="Battle of Curzola">battle of Curzola</a> (September 1298), off the Dalmatian coast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuljiz-Šostik201528–36_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuljiz-Šostik201528–36-42">[42]</a></sup> a claim which is due to a later tradition (16th century) recorded by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Ramusio" title="Giovanni Battista Ramusio">Giovanni Battista Ramusio</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPuljiz-Šostik20158,_12,_28–36_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPuljiz-Šostik20158,_12,_28–36-44">[44]</a></sup>).
</p><p>He spent several months of his imprisonment dictating a detailed account of his travels to a fellow inmate, <a href="/wiki/Rustichello_da_Pisa" title="Rustichello da Pisa">Rustichello da Pisa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup> who incorporated tales of his own as well as other collected anecdotes and current affairs from China. The book soon spread throughout Europe in <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscript</a> form, and became known as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">The Travels of Marco Polo</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> title: <i><b>Il Milione</b></i>, lit. "The Million", deriving from Polo's nickname "Milione". Original title in <a href="/wiki/Franco-Italian" title="Franco-Italian">Franco-Italian</a> : <i>Livres des Merveilles du Monde</i>). It depicts the Polos' journeys throughout Asia, giving Europeans their first comprehensive look into the inner workings of the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>, including China, India, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Polo was finally released from captivity in August 1299,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup> and returned home to Venice, where his father and uncle in the meantime had purchased a large <a href="/wiki/Palace#Italy" title="Palace">palazzo</a> in the zone named <i>contrada San Giovanni Crisostomo</i> (Corte del Milion).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007332_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007332-46">[46]</a></sup> For such a venture, the Polo family probably invested profits from trading, and even many gemstones they brought from the East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007332_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007332-46">[46]</a></sup> The company continued its activities and Marco soon became a wealthy merchant. Marco and his uncle Maffeo financed other expeditions, but likely never left Venetian provinces, nor returned to the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007333_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007333-47">[47]</a></sup> Sometime before 1300, his father Niccolò died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007333_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007333-47">[47]</a></sup> In 1300, he married Donata Badoèr, the daughter of Vitale Badoèr, a merchant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007332–33_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007332–33-48">[48]</a></sup> They had three daughters, Fantina (married Marco Bragadin), Bellela (married Bertuccio Querini), and Moreta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007333,_338_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007333,_338-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pietro_d%27Abano" title="Pietro d'Abano">Pietro d'Abano</a> philosopher, doctor and astrologer based in <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a>, reports having spoken with Marco Polo about what he had observed in the vault of the sky during his travels. Marco told him that during his return trip to the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>, he had spotted what he describes in a drawing as a star "shaped like a sack" (in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>: <i>ut sacco</i>) with a big tail (<i>magna habens caudam</i>), most likely a <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a>. Astronomers agree that there were no comets sighted in Europe at the end of the thirteenth century, but there are records about a comet sighted in China and Indonesia in 1293.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rhinoresourcecenter.com_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-rhinoresourcecenter.com-51">[51]</a></sup> Interestingly, this circumstance does not appear in <a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">Polo's book of Travels</a>. Peter D'Abano kept the drawing in his volume "Conciliator Differentiarum, quæ inter Philosophos et Medicos Versantur". Marco Polo gave Pietro other astronomical observations he made in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>, and also a description of the <a href="/wiki/Sumatran_rhinoceros" title="Sumatran rhinoceros">Sumatran rhinoceros</a>, which are collected in the <i>Conciliator</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rhinoresourcecenter.com_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-rhinoresourcecenter.com-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1305 he is mentioned in a Venetian document among local sea captains regarding the payment of taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljica_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Poljica-26">[26]</a></sup> His relation with a certain Marco Polo, who in 1300 was mentioned with riots against the aristocratic government, and escaped the death penalty, as well as riots from 1310 led by <a href="/wiki/Bajamonte_Tiepolo" title="Bajamonte Tiepolo">Bajamonte Tiepolo</a> and Marco Querini, among whose rebels were Jacobello and Francesco Polo from another family branch, is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljica_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-Poljica-26">[26]</a></sup> Polo is clearly mentioned again after 1305 in Maffeo's testament from 1309–1310, in a 1319 document according to which he became owner of some estates of his deceased father, and in 1321, when he bought part of the family property of his wife Donata.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljica_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-Poljica-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chiesa_di_San_Lorenzo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4913" data-file-width="3950" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Chiesa_di_San_Lorenzo.jpg/220px-Chiesa_di_San_Lorenzo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Chiesa_di_San_Lorenzo.jpg/330px-Chiesa_di_San_Lorenzo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Chiesa_di_San_Lorenzo.jpg/440px-Chiesa_di_San_Lorenzo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chiesa_di_San_Lorenzo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/San_Lorenzo,_Venice" title="San Lorenzo, Venice">San Lorenzo</a></i> church in the <a href="/wiki/Sestiere" title="Sestiere">sestiere</a> of <a href="/wiki/Castello,_Venice" title="Castello, Venice">Castello</a> (<a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>), where Polo was buried. The photo shows the church as it is today, after the 1592 rebuilding.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venezia,_casa_di_marco_polo_della_corte_del_milion_vista_dal_canale,_targa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="876" data-file-width="1284" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Venezia%2C_casa_di_marco_polo_della_corte_del_milion_vista_dal_canale%2C_targa.JPG/220px-Venezia%2C_casa_di_marco_polo_della_corte_del_milion_vista_dal_canale%2C_targa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Venezia%2C_casa_di_marco_polo_della_corte_del_milion_vista_dal_canale%2C_targa.JPG/330px-Venezia%2C_casa_di_marco_polo_della_corte_del_milion_vista_dal_canale%2C_targa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Venezia%2C_casa_di_marco_polo_della_corte_del_milion_vista_dal_canale%2C_targa.JPG/440px-Venezia%2C_casa_di_marco_polo_della_corte_del_milion_vista_dal_canale%2C_targa.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venezia,_casa_di_marco_polo_della_corte_del_milion_vista_dal_canale,_targa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plaque on <a href="/wiki/Teatro_Malibran" title="Teatro Malibran">Teatro Malibran</a>, which was built upon Marco Polo's house</div></div></div>
<p>In 1323, Polo was confined to bed, due to illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007339_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007339-52">[52]</a></sup> On 8 January 1324, despite physicians' efforts to treat him, Polo was on his deathbed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340-53">[53]</a></sup> To write and certify the will, his family requested Giovanni Giustiniani, a priest of San Procolo. His wife, Donata, and his three daughters were appointed by him as <a href="/wiki/Executor" title="Executor">co-executrices</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340-53">[53]</a></sup> The church was entitled by law to a portion of his estate; he approved of this and ordered that a further sum be paid to the convent of <a href="/wiki/San_Lorenzo,_Venice" title="San Lorenzo, Venice">San Lorenzo</a>, the place where he wished to be buried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340-53">[53]</a></sup> He also set free Peter, a <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tartar</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave" title="Slave">servant</a>, who may have accompanied him from Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and to whom Polo bequeathed 100 lire of Venetian denari.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007341_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007341-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>He divided up the rest of his assets, including several properties, among individuals, religious institutions, and every guild and fraternity to which he belonged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340-53">[53]</a></sup> He also wrote off multiple debts including 300 lire that his sister-in-law owed him, and others for the convent of <a href="/wiki/San_Giovanni_Grisostomo,_Venice" title="San Giovanni Grisostomo, Venice">San Giovanni</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_of_San_Polo,_Venice" title="Church of San Polo, Venice">San Paolo of the Order of Preachers</a>, and a cleric named <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">Friar</a> Benvenuto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340-53">[53]</a></sup> He ordered 220 <a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">soldi</a> be paid to Giovanni Giustiniani for his work as a notary and his prayers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340–41_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007340–41-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The will was not signed by Polo, but was validated by the then-relevant "<a href="/wiki/Signum_manus" title="Signum manus">signum manus</a>" rule, by which the testator only had to touch the document to make it legally valid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007341_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007341-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marciana_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marciana-57">[57]</a></sup> Due to the Venetian law stating that the day ends at sunset, the exact date of Marco Polo's death cannot be determined, but according to some scholars it was between the sunsets of 8 and 9 January 1324.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007342_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBergreen2007342-58">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Marciana" title="Biblioteca Marciana">Biblioteca Marciana</a>, which holds the original copy of his testament, dates the testament on 9 January 1323, and gives the date of his death at some time in June 1324.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marciana_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marciana-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo"><i>The Travels of Marco Polo</i></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">The Travels of Marco Polo</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Franco-Mongol_alliance" title="Franco-Mongol alliance">Franco-Mongol alliance</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine-Mongol_alliance" title="Byzantine-Mongol alliance">Byzantine-Mongol alliance</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Route_of_Marco_Polo.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="795" data-file-width="1123" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Route_of_Marco_Polo.png/230px-Route_of_Marco_Polo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Route_of_Marco_Polo.png/345px-Route_of_Marco_Polo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Route_of_Marco_Polo.png/460px-Route_of_Marco_Polo.png 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Map of Marco Polo's travels</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Polo_traveling.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2006" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Marco_Polo_traveling.JPG/230px-Marco_Polo_traveling.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Marco_Polo_traveling.JPG/345px-Marco_Polo_traveling.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Marco_Polo_traveling.JPG/460px-Marco_Polo_traveling.JPG 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A <a href="/wiki/Miniature_(illuminated_manuscript)" title="Miniature (illuminated manuscript)">miniature</a> from <i>Il Milione</i></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>An authoritative version of Marco Polo's book does not and cannot exist, for the early manuscripts differ significantly, and the reconstruction of the original text is a matter of <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">textual criticism</a>. A total of about 150 copies in various languages are known to exist. Before the availability of <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, errors were frequently made during copying and translating, so there are many differences between the various copies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKellogg2001_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKellogg2001-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAG_p.1_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAG_p.1-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Polo related his memoirs orally to <a href="/wiki/Rustichello_da_Pisa" title="Rustichello da Pisa">Rustichello da Pisa</a> while both were prisoners of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genova_Republic" title="Genova Republic">Genova Republic</a>. Rustichello wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">Devisement du Monde</a></i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franco-Venetian" title="Franco-Venetian">Franco-Venetian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nota_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nota-61">[61]</a></sup> The idea probably was to create a <a href="/wiki/Pratica_della_mercatura" title="Pratica della mercatura">handbook for merchants</a>, essentially a text on weights, measures and distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest surviving manuscript is in <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> heavily flavoured with Italian;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> According to the Italian scholar Luigi Foscolo Benedetto, this "F" text is the basic original text, which he corrected by comparing it with the somewhat more detailed Italian of Giovanni Battista Ramusio, together with a Latin manuscript in the <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Ambrosiana" title="Biblioteca Ambrosiana">Biblioteca Ambrosiana</a>.  Other early important sources are R (Ramusio's Italian translation first printed in 1559), and Z (a fifteenth-century Latin manuscript kept at Toledo, Spain). Another Old French Polo manuscript, dating to around 1350, is held by the National Library of Sweden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the early manuscripts <i>Iter Marci Pauli Veneti</i> was a translation into Latin made by the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican brother</a> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Francesco_Pipino&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Francesco Pipino (page does not exist)">Francesco Pipino</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francesco_Pipino" title="it:Francesco Pipino">it</a>]</span> in 1302, just a few years after Marco's return to Venice. Since Latin was then the most widespread and authoritative language of culture, it is suggested that Rustichello's text was translated into Latin for a precise will of the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican Order</a>, and this helped to promote the book on a European scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unive.it_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-unive.it-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first English translation is the Elizabethan version by <a href="/wiki/John_Frampton" title="John Frampton">John Frampton</a> published in 1579, <i>The most noble and famous travels of Marco Polo</i>, based on Santaella's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Spanish_language" title="Old Spanish language">Castilian</a> translation of 1503 (the first version in that language).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The published editions of Polo's book rely on single manuscripts, blend multiple versions together, or add notes to clarify, for example in the English translation by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Yule" title="Henry Yule">Henry Yule</a>. The 1938 English translation by <span style="white-space: nowrap;">A. C.</span> Moule and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Pelliot" title="Paul Pelliot">Paul Pelliot</a> is based on a Latin manuscript found in the library of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Toledo" title="Cathedral of Toledo">Cathedral of Toledo</a> in 1932, and is 50% longer than other versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-book_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-book-66">[66]</a></sup> The popular translation published by Penguin Books in 1958 by <a href="/wiki/R._E._Latham" title="R. E. Latham"> <span style="white-space: nowrap;">R. E.</span> Latham</a> works several texts together to make a readable whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Narrative">Narrative</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MarcoPoloStatueInHangzhou.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="353" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/MarcoPoloStatueInHangzhou.JPG/140px-MarcoPoloStatueInHangzhou.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/MarcoPoloStatueInHangzhou.JPG/210px-MarcoPoloStatueInHangzhou.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/MarcoPoloStatueInHangzhou.JPG/280px-MarcoPoloStatueInHangzhou.JPG 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MarcoPoloStatueInHangzhou.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Marco Polo in Hangzhou, China</div></div></div>
<p>The book opens with a preface describing his father and uncle travelling to <a href="/wiki/Bolghar" title="Bolghar">Bolghar</a> where Prince <a href="/wiki/Berke" title="Berke">Berke Khan</a> lived. A year later, they went to <a href="/wiki/Ukek" title="Ukek">Ukek</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YuleCH2_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-YuleCH2-68">[68]</a></sup> and continued to <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a>. There, an envoy from the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> invited them to meet <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, who had never met Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In 1266, they reached the seat of Kublai Khan at <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Dadu</a>, present-day <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, China. Kublai received the brothers with hospitality and asked them many questions regarding the European legal and political system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> He also inquired about the Pope and Church in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> After the brothers answered the questions he tasked them with delivering a letter to the Pope, requesting 100 Christians acquainted with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" title="Liberal arts">Seven Arts</a> (grammar, rhetoric, logic, geometry, arithmetic, music and astronomy). Kublai Khan requested also that an envoy bring him back <a href="/wiki/Chrism" title="Chrism">oil of the lamp in Jerusalem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The long <i><a href="/wiki/Sede_vacante" title="Sede vacante">sede vacante</a></i> between the death of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_IV" title="Pope Clement IV">Pope Clement IV</a> in 1268 and the election of his successor delayed the Polos in fulfilling Kublai's request. They followed the suggestion of Theobald Visconti, then <a href="/wiki/Papal_legate" title="Papal legate">papal legate</a> for the realm of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, and returned to Venice in 1269 or 1270 to await the nomination of the new Pope, which allowed Marco to see his father for the first time, at the age of fifteen or sixteen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YuleCH9_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-YuleCH9-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kublai_Khan_meeting_Marco_Polo.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="269" data-file-width="432" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Kublai_Khan_meeting_Marco_Polo.png/220px-Kublai_Khan_meeting_Marco_Polo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Kublai_Khan_meeting_Marco_Polo.png/330px-Kublai_Khan_meeting_Marco_Polo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Kublai_Khan_meeting_Marco_Polo.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kublai_Khan_meeting_Marco_Polo.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Polo meeting <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1271, Niccolò, Maffeo and Marco Polo embarked on their voyage to fulfil Kublai's request. They sailed to <a href="/wiki/Acre,_Israel" title="Acre, Israel">Acre</a>, and then rode on camels to the Persian port of <a href="/wiki/Hormuz,_Iran" title="Hormuz, Iran">Hormuz</a>. The Polos wanted to sail straight into China, but the ships there were not seaworthy, so they continued overland through the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, until reaching Kublai's summer palace in <a href="/wiki/Shangdu" title="Shangdu">Shangdu</a>, near present-day <a href="/wiki/Zhangjiakou" title="Zhangjiakou">Zhangjiakou</a>. In one instance during their trip, the Polos joined a caravan of travelling merchants whom they crossed paths with. Unfortunately, the party was soon attacked by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bandits" title="Bandits">bandits</a>, who used the cover of a sandstorm to ambush them. The Polos managed to fight and escape through a nearby town, but many members of the caravan were killed or enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Three and a half years after leaving Venice, when Marco was about 21 years old, the Polos were welcomed by Kublai into his palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup> The exact date of their arrival is unknown, but scholars estimate it to be between 1271 and 1275.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[nb 1]</a></sup> On reaching the Yuan court, the Polos presented the sacred oil from Jerusalem and the papal letters to their patron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica571_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica571-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marco knew four languages, and the family had accumulated a great deal of knowledge and experience that was useful to Kublai. It is possible that he became a government official;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup> he wrote about many imperial visits to China's southern and eastern provinces, the far south and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> They were highly respected and sought after in the Mongolian court, and so Kublai Khan decided to decline the Polos' requests to leave China. They became worried about returning home safely, believing that if Kublai died, his enemies might turn against them because of their close involvement with the ruler. In 1292, Kublai's great-nephew, then ruler of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persia</a>, sent representatives to China in search of a potential wife, and they asked the Polos to accompany them, so they were permitted to return to Persia with the wedding party—which left that same year from <a href="/wiki/Quanzhou" title="Quanzhou">Zaitun</a> in southern China on a fleet of 14 <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">junks</a>. The party sailed to the port of <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> travelled north to <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> and around the southern tip of India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> eventually crossing the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ormus" title="Ormus">Hormuz</a>. The two-year voyage was a perilous one—of the six hundred people (not including the crew) in the convoy only eighteen had survived (including all three Polos).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> The Polos left the wedding party after reaching Hormuz and travelled overland to the port of Trebizond on the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, the present-day <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Trabzon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_28-9"><a href="#cite_note-WB-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Polo,_Il_Milione,_Chapter_CXXIII_and_CXXIV_Cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1170" data-file-width="763" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Marco_Polo%2C_Il_Milione%2C_Chapter_CXXIII_and_CXXIV_Cropped.jpg/170px-Marco_Polo%2C_Il_Milione%2C_Chapter_CXXIII_and_CXXIV_Cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Marco_Polo%2C_Il_Milione%2C_Chapter_CXXIII_and_CXXIV_Cropped.jpg/255px-Marco_Polo%2C_Il_Milione%2C_Chapter_CXXIII_and_CXXIV_Cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Marco_Polo%2C_Il_Milione%2C_Chapter_CXXIII_and_CXXIV_Cropped.jpg/340px-Marco_Polo%2C_Il_Milione%2C_Chapter_CXXIII_and_CXXIV_Cropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Polo,_Il_Milione,_Chapter_CXXIII_and_CXXIV_Cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from <i>Il Milione</i>, from a manuscript believed to date between 1298 and 1299</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_Rustichello">Role of Rustichello</span></h3>
<p>The British scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ronald_Latham" title="Ronald Latham">Ronald Latham</a> has pointed out that <i>The Book of Marvels</i> was, in fact, a collaboration written in 1298–1299 between Polo and a professional writer of romances, Rustichello of Pisa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Latham_pages_7_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Latham_pages_7-81">[80]</a></sup> It is believed that Polo related his memoirs orally to <a href="/wiki/Rustichello_da_Pisa" title="Rustichello da Pisa">Rustichello da Pisa</a> while both were prisoners of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genova_Republic" title="Genova Republic">Genova Republic</a>. Rustichello wrote <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Il_Milione" title="Il Milione">Devisement du Monde</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Franco-Italian" title="Franco-Italian">Franco-Venetian language</a>, which was the language of culture widespread in northern Italy between the subalpine belt and the lower Po between the 13th and 15th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nota_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nota-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Latham also argued that Rustichello may have glamorised Polo's accounts, and added fantastic and romantic elements that made the book a bestseller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Latham_pages_7_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Latham_pages_7-81">[80]</a></sup> The Italian scholar Luigi Foscolo Benedetto had previously demonstrated that the book was written in the same "leisurely, conversational style" that characterised Rustichello's other works, and that some passages in the book were taken verbatim or with minimal modifications from other writings by Rustichello. For example, the opening introduction in <i>The Book of Marvels</i> to "emperors and kings, dukes and marquises" was lifted straight out of an <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">Arthurian romance</a> Rustichello had written several years earlier, and the account of the second meeting between Polo and Kublai Khan at the latter's court is almost the same as that of the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Tristan" title="Tristan">Tristan</a> at the court of <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a> at <a href="/wiki/Camelot" title="Camelot">Camelot</a> in that same book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> Latham believed that many elements of the book, such as legends of the Middle East and mentions of exotic marvels, may have been the work of Rustichello who was giving what medieval European readers expected to find in a travel book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_the_Dominican_Order">Role of the Dominican Order</span></h3>
<p>Apparently, from the very beginning, Marco's story aroused contrasting reactions, as it was received by some with a certain disbelief. The <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican father</a> Francesco Pipino was the author of a translation into Latin,  <i>Iter Marci Pauli Veneti</i> in 1302, just a few years after Marco's return to Venice. Francesco Pipino solemnly affirmed the truthfulness of the book and defined Marco as a "prudent, honoured and faithful man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rinaldo_Fulin_1924,_p._255_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rinaldo_Fulin_1924,_p._255-85">[84]</a></sup>
In his writings, the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican brother</a>  Jacopo d'Acqui explains why his contemporaries were sceptical about the content of the book. He also relates that before dying, Marco Polo insisted that "he had told only a half of the things he had seen".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rinaldo_Fulin_1924,_p._255_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rinaldo_Fulin_1924,_p._255-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to some recent research of the Italian scholar Antonio Montefusco, the very close relationship that Marco Polo cultivated with members of the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican Order</a> in Venice suggests that local fathers collaborated with him for a Latin version of the book, which means that Rustichello's text was translated into Latin for a precise will of the Order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unive.it_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-unive.it-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since Dominican fathers had among their <a href="/wiki/Christian_mission" title="Christian mission">missions</a> that of evangelizing foreign peoples (cf. the role of Dominican missionaries in China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> and in the Indies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup>), it is reasonable to think that they considered Marco's book as a trustworthy piece of information for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_(station)" title="Mission (station)">missions</a> in the East. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diplomatic_communications" title="Diplomatic communications">diplomatic communications</a> between <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">Pope Innocent IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_X" title="Pope Gregory X">Pope Gregory X</a> with the Mongols<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> were probably another reason for this endorsement. At the time, there was open discussion of a possible Christian-Mongol alliance with an anti-Islamic function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richard-465_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-richard-465-89">[88]</a></sup> In fact, a Mongol delegate was solemny baptised at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Council_of_Lyon" title="Second Council of Lyon">Second Council of Lyon</a>. At the council, Pope Gregory X promulgated a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crusade" title="Crusade">Crusade</a> to start in 1278 in liaison with the Mongols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authenticity_and_veracity">Authenticity and veracity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BNF_Fr2810_f136v_Frontispice_Grant_Kaan_de_Cathay.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1366" data-file-width="966" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/BNF_Fr2810_f136v_Frontispice_Grant_Kaan_de_Cathay.jpeg/170px-BNF_Fr2810_f136v_Frontispice_Grant_Kaan_de_Cathay.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/BNF_Fr2810_f136v_Frontispice_Grant_Kaan_de_Cathay.jpeg/255px-BNF_Fr2810_f136v_Frontispice_Grant_Kaan_de_Cathay.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/BNF_Fr2810_f136v_Frontispice_Grant_Kaan_de_Cathay.jpeg/340px-BNF_Fr2810_f136v_Frontispice_Grant_Kaan_de_Cathay.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BNF_Fr2810_f136v_Frontispice_Grant_Kaan_de_Cathay.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kublai Khan's court, from the French "Livre des merveilles"</div></div></div>
<p>Since its publication, some have viewed the book with skepticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chang_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-chang-91">[90]</a></sup> Some in the Middle Ages regarded the book simply as a romance or fable, due largely to the sharp difference of its descriptions of a sophisticated civilisation in China to other early accounts by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_da_Pian_del_Carpine" title="Giovanni da Pian del Carpine">Giovanni da Pian del Carpine</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_of_Rubruck" title="William of Rubruck">William of Rubruck</a>, who portrayed the Mongols as '<a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarians</a>' who appeared to belong to 'some other world'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chang_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-chang-91">[90]</a></sup> Doubts have also been raised in later centuries about Marco Polo's narrative of his travels in China, for example for his failure to mention the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a>, and in particular the difficulties in identifying many of the place names he used<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw1_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-haw1-92">[91]</a></sup> (the great majority, however, have since been identified).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> Many have questioned whether he had visited the places he mentioned in his itinerary, whether he had appropriated the accounts of his father and uncle or other travellers, and some doubted whether he even reached China, or that if he did, perhaps never went beyond <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a> (Beijing).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw1_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-haw1-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haeger_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-haeger-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has, however, been pointed out that Polo's accounts of China are more accurate and detailed than other travellers' accounts of the period. Polo had at times refuted the 'marvellous' fables and legends given in other European accounts, and despite some exaggerations and errors, Polo's accounts have relatively few of the descriptions of irrational marvels. In many cases of descriptions of events where he was not present (mostly given in the first part before he reached China, such as mentions of Christian miracles), he made a clear distinction that they are what he had heard rather than what he had seen.  It is also largely free of the gross errors found in other accounts such as those given by the Moroccan traveller <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a> who had confused the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_(China)" title="Grand Canal (China)">Grand Canal</a> and other waterways, and believed that <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> was made from coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern studies have further shown that details given in Marco Polo's book, such as the currencies used, salt productions and revenues, are accurate and unique. Such detailed descriptions are not found in other non-Chinese sources, and their accuracy is supported by archaeological evidence as well as Chinese records compiled after Polo had left China. His accounts are therefore unlikely to have been obtained second hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vogel_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-vogel-96">[95]</a></sup> Other accounts have also been verified; for example, when visiting <a href="/wiki/Zhenjiang" title="Zhenjiang">Zhenjiang</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, China, Marco Polo noted that a large number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_church" title="Christian church">Christian churches</a> had been built there. His claim is confirmed by a Chinese text of the 14th century explaining how a <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> named Mar-Sargis from <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> founded six <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian Christian churches</a> there in addition to one in <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a> during the second half of the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> His story of the princess <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6k%C3%B6chin" title="Kököchin">Kököchin</a> sent from China to Persia to marry the Īl-khān is also confirmed by independent sources in both Persia and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cleaves_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-cleaves-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_analyses">Scholarly analyses</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LetterInnocenceToTartarKingAndPeople_a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1223" data-file-width="853" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/LetterInnocenceToTartarKingAndPeople_a.jpg/170px-LetterInnocenceToTartarKingAndPeople_a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/LetterInnocenceToTartarKingAndPeople_a.jpg/255px-LetterInnocenceToTartarKingAndPeople_a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/LetterInnocenceToTartarKingAndPeople_a.jpg/340px-LetterInnocenceToTartarKingAndPeople_a.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LetterInnocenceToTartarKingAndPeople_a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Text of the letter of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">Pope Innocent IV</a> "to the ruler and people of the Tartars", brought to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%BCy%C3%BCg_Khan" title="Güyüg Khan">Güyüg Khan</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_de_Carpini" title="John de Carpini">John de Carpini</a>, 1245</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guyuk_khan%27s_Stamp_1246.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="434" data-file-width="434" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Guyuk_khan%27s_Stamp_1246.jpg/170px-Guyuk_khan%27s_Stamp_1246.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Guyuk_khan%27s_Stamp_1246.jpg/255px-Guyuk_khan%27s_Stamp_1246.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Guyuk_khan%27s_Stamp_1246.jpg/340px-Guyuk_khan%27s_Stamp_1246.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guyuk_khan%27s_Stamp_1246.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seal of <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_Khan" title="Güyük Khan">Güyük Khan</a> using the classical <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_script" title="Mongolian script">Mongolian script</a>, as found in a letter sent to the Roman <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">Pope Innocent IV</a> in 1246</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LetterArghunToNicholasIV1290VaticanArchives.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="639" data-file-width="2556" decoding="async" height="43" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/LetterArghunToNicholasIV1290VaticanArchives.jpg/170px-LetterArghunToNicholasIV1290VaticanArchives.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/LetterArghunToNicholasIV1290VaticanArchives.jpg/255px-LetterArghunToNicholasIV1290VaticanArchives.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/LetterArghunToNicholasIV1290VaticanArchives.jpg/340px-LetterArghunToNicholasIV1290VaticanArchives.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LetterArghunToNicholasIV1290VaticanArchives.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Letter from <a href="/wiki/Arghun" title="Arghun">Arghun</a>, Khan of the Mongol <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_IV" title="Pope Nicholas IV">Pope Nicholas IV</a>, 1290</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GhazanSeal1302LetterToBonifaceVIII.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="902" data-file-width="875" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/GhazanSeal1302LetterToBonifaceVIII.JPG/170px-GhazanSeal1302LetterToBonifaceVIII.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/GhazanSeal1302LetterToBonifaceVIII.JPG/255px-GhazanSeal1302LetterToBonifaceVIII.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/GhazanSeal1302LetterToBonifaceVIII.JPG/340px-GhazanSeal1302LetterToBonifaceVIII.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GhazanSeal1302LetterToBonifaceVIII.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seal of the Mongol ruler <a href="/wiki/Ghazan" title="Ghazan">Ghazan</a> in a 1302 letter to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Boniface_VIII" title="Pope Boniface VIII">Pope Boniface VIII</a>, with an inscription in Chinese <a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">seal script</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Explaining_omissions">Explaining omissions</span></h3>
<p>Sceptics have long wondered whether Marco Polo wrote his book based on hearsay, with some pointing to omissions about noteworthy practices and structures of China as well as the lack of details on some places in his book. While Polo describes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_money" title="Paper money">paper money</a> and the burning of coal, he fails to mention the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chopstick" title="Chopstick">chopsticks</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Footbinding" title="Footbinding">footbinding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> His failure to note the presence of the Great Wall of China was first raised in the middle of the seventeenth century, and in the middle of the eighteenth century, it was suggested that he might have never reached China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw1_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-haw1-92">[91]</a></sup>  Later scholars such as John W. Haeger argued that Marco Polo might not have visited Southern China due to the lack of details in his description of southern Chinese cities compared to northern ones, while <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Franke_(sinologist)" title="Herbert Franke (sinologist)">Herbert Franke</a> also raised the possibility that Marco Polo might not have been to China at all, and wondered if he might have based his accounts on Persian sources due to his use of Persian expressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haeger_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-haeger-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-franke_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-franke-100">[99]</a></sup> This is taken further by <a href="/wiki/Frances_Wood" title="Frances Wood">Frances Wood</a> who claimed in her 1995 book <i><a href="/wiki/Did_Marco_Polo_Go_to_China%3F" title="Did Marco Polo Go to China?">Did Marco Polo Go to China?</a></i> that at best Polo never went farther east than Persia (modern Iran), and that there is nothing in <i>The Book of Marvels</i> about China that could not be obtained via reading Persian books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan,_D_page_222_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan,_D_page_222-101">[100]</a></sup> Wood maintains that it is more probable that Polo only went to Constantinople (modern Istanbul, Turkey) and some of the Italian merchant colonies around the Black Sea, picking hearsay from those travellers who had been farther east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan,_D_page_222_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan,_D_page_222-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Supporters of Polo's basic accuracy countered on the points raised by sceptics such as footbinding and the Great Wall of China. Historian <a href="/wiki/Stephen_G._Haw" title="Stephen G. Haw">Stephen G. Haw</a> argued that the Great Walls were built to keep out northern invaders, whereas the ruling dynasty during Marco Polo's visit were those very northern invaders.
