They note that the Great Wall familiar to us today is a <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a> structure built some two centuries after Marco Polo's travels; and that the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Mongol</a> rulers whom Polo served controlled territories both north and south of today's wall, and would have no reasons to maintain any fortifications that may have remained there from the earlier dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polo_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-polo-102">[101]</a></sup> Other Europeans who travelled to <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a> during the Yuan dynasty, such as <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_de%27_Marignolli" title="Giovanni de' Marignolli">Giovanni de' Marignolli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Odoric_of_Pordenone" title="Odoric of Pordenone">Odoric of Pordenone</a>, said nothing about the wall either. The Muslim traveller <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a>, who asked about the wall when he visited China during the Yuan dynasty, could find no one who had either seen it or knew of anyone who had seen it, suggesting that while ruins of the wall constructed in the earlier periods might have existed, they were not significant or noteworthy at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polo_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-polo-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Haw also argued that footbinding was not common even among Chinese during Polo's time and almost unknown among the Mongols. While the Italian missionary <a href="/wiki/Odoric_of_Pordenone" title="Odoric of Pordenone">Odoric of Pordenone</a> who visited <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan</a> China mentioned footbinding (it is however unclear whether he was merely relaying something he had heard as his description is inaccurate),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> no other foreign visitors to <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan</a> China mentioned the practice, perhaps an indication that the footbinding was not widespread or was not practised in an extreme form at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup> Marco Polo himself noted (in the Toledo manuscript) the dainty walk of Chinese women who took very short steps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-polo_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-polo-102">[101]</a></sup> It has also been noted by other scholars that many of the things not mentioned by Marco Polo such as tea and chopsticks were not mentioned by other travellers as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Igor_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Igor-38">[38]</a></sup>  Haw also pointed out that despite the few omissions, Marco Polo's account is more extensive, more accurate and more detailed than those of other foreign travellers to China in this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> Marco Polo even observed Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nautical" title="Nautical">nautical</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_inventions" title="List of Chinese inventions">inventions</a> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watertight_compartment" title="Watertight compartment">watertight compartments</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bulkhead_(partition)" title="Bulkhead (partition)">bulkhead partitions</a> in <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">Chinese ships</a>, knowledge of which he was keen to share with his fellow Venetians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to Haw, a number of other scholars have argued in favour of the established view that Polo was in China in response to Wood's book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Igor_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Igor-38">[38]</a></sup> The book has been criticized by figures including <a href="/wiki/Igor_de_Rachewiltz" title="Igor de Rachewiltz">Igor de Rachewiltz</a> (translator and annotator of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i>) and Morris Rossabi (author of <i>Kublai Khan: his life and times</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> The historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Morgan_(historian)" title="David Morgan (historian)">David Morgan</a> points out basic errors made in Wood's book such as confusing the <a href="/wiki/Liao_dynasty" title="Liao dynasty">Liao dynasty</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty</a>, and he found no compelling evidence in the book that would convince him that Marco Polo did not go to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan,_D_page_224_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan,_D_page_224-108">[107]</a></sup> Haw also argues in his book <i>Marco Polo's China</i> that Marco's account is much more correct and accurate than has often been supposed and that it is extremely unlikely that he could have obtained all the information in his book from second-hand sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> Haw also criticizes Wood's approach to finding mention of Marco Polo in Chinese texts by contending that contemporaneous Europeans had little regard for using <a href="/wiki/Surname#history" title="Surname">surnames</a> and that a direct Chinese <a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">transliteration</a> of the name "Marco" ignores the possibility of him taking on a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_name" title="Chinese name">Chinese</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_name" title="Mongolian name">Mongol name</a> with no similarity to his <a href="/wiki/Christian_name" title="Christian name">Latin name</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also in reply to Wood, Jørgen Jensen recalled the meeting of Marco Polo and <a href="/wiki/Pietro_d%27Abano" title="Pietro d'Abano">Pietro d'Abano</a> in the late 13th century. During this meeting, Marco gave to Pietro details of the astronomical observations he had made on his journey. These observations are only compatible with Marco's stay in China, <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a> and the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> and are recorded in Pietro's book <i>Conciliator Differentiarum</i>, but not in Marco's <i>Book of Travels</i>.
</p><p>Reviewing Haw's book, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jackson_(historian)" title="Peter Jackson (historian)">Peter Jackson</a> (author of <i>The Mongols and the West</i>) has said that Haw "must surely now have settled the controversy surrounding the historicity of Polo's visit to China".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> Igor de Rachewiltz's review, which refutes Wood's points, concludes with a strongly-worded condemnation: "I regret to say that F. W.'s book falls short of the standard of scholarship that one would expect in a work of this kind. Her book can only be described as deceptive, both in relation to the author and to the public at large. Questions are posted that, in the majority of cases, have already been answered satisfactorily ... her attempt is unprofessional; she is poorly equipped in the basic tools of the trade, i.e., adequate linguistic competence and research methodology ... and her major arguments cannot withstand close scrutiny. Her conclusion fails to consider all the evidence supporting Marco Polo's credibility."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allegations_of_exaggeration">Allegations of exaggeration</span></h3>
<p>Some scholars believe that Marco Polo exaggerated his importance in China. The British historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Morgan_(historian)" title="David Morgan (historian)">David Morgan</a> thought that Polo had likely exaggerated and lied about his status in China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan,_D_page_223_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan,_D_page_223-114">[113]</a></sup> while Ronald Latham believed that such exaggerations were embellishments by his ghostwriter <a href="/wiki/Rustichello_da_Pisa" title="Rustichello da Pisa">Rustichello da Pisa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Et meser Marc Pol meisme, celui de cui trate ceste livre, seingneurie ceste cité por trois anz.</i></span><br/>
</p><p><span title="English-language text"><span lang="en">And the same Marco Polo, of whom this book relates, ruled this city for three years.</span></span>
</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a class="external text" href="https://edizionicafoscari.unive.it/media/pdf/books/978-88-6969-224-6/978-88-6969-224-6_69TdyXc.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>Le divisement dou monde, CXLII</i>, ed. Mario Eusebi, p. 162</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>This sentence in <i>The Book of Marvels</i> was interpreted as Marco Polo was "the governor" of the city of "Yangiu" <a href="/wiki/Yangzhou" title="Yangzhou">Yangzhou</a> for three years, and later of <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a>. This claim has raised some controversy. According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Morgan_(historian)" title="David Morgan (historian)">David Morgan</a>  no Chinese source mentions him as either a friend of the Emperor or as the governor of Yangzhou – indeed no Chinese source mentions Marco Polo at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan,_D_page_223_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan,_D_page_223-114">[113]</a></sup> In fact, in the 1960s the German historian <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Franke_(sinologist)" title="Herbert Franke (sinologist)">Herbert Franke</a> noted that all occurrences of Po-lo or Bolod in Yuan texts were names of people of Mongol or Turkic extraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-franke_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-franke-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, in the 2010s the Chinese scholar <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peng_Hai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peng Hai (page does not exist)">Peng Hai</a> identified Marco Polo with a certain "Boluo", a courtier of the emperor, who is mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Yuan" title="History of Yuan">Yuanshi</a> ("History of Yuan") since he was arrested in 1274 by an imperial dignitary named Saman. The accusation was that Boluo had walked on the same side of the road as a female courtesan, in contravention of the order for men and women to walk on opposite sides of the road inside the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> According to the "Yuanshi" records, Boluo was released at the request of the emperor himself, and was then transferred to the region of Ningxia, in the northeast of present-day China, in the spring of 1275. The date could correspond to the first mission of which Marco Polo speaks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>If this identification is correct, there is a record about Marco Polo in Chinese sources. These conjectures seem to be supported by the fact that in addition to the imperial dignitary Saman (the one who had arrested the official named "Boluo"), the documents mention his brother, Xiangwei. According to sources, Saman died shortly after the incident, while Xiangwei was transferred to Yangzhou in 1282–1283. Marco Polo reports that he was moved to Hangzhou the following year, in 1284. It has been supposed that these displacements are due to the intention to avoid further conflicts between the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Busi_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Busi-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sinologist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Pelliot" title="Paul Pelliot">Paul Pelliot</a> thought that Polo might have served as an officer of the government salt monopoly in Yangzhou, which was a position of some significance that could explain the exaggeration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan,_D_page_223_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan,_D_page_223-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>It may seem unlikely that a European could hold a position of power in the Mongolian empire. However, some records prove he was not the first nor the only one. In his book, Marco mentions an official named "Mar Sarchis" who probably was a <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishop</a>, and he says he founded two Christian churches in the region of "Caigiu". This official is actually mentioned in the local gazette <i>Zhishun Zhenjian zhi</i> under the name "Ma Xuelijisi" and the qualification of "General of Third Class". Always in the gazette, it is said Ma Xuelijsi was an assistant supervisor in the province of Zhenjiang for three years, and that during this time he founded two Christian churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vogel_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vogel-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Busi_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Busi-117">[116]</a></sup> In fact, it is a well-documented fact that <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> trusted foreigners more than Chinese subjects in internal affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vogel:2_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vogel:2-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Busi_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-Busi-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stephen_G._Haw" title="Stephen G. Haw">Stephen G. Haw</a> challenges this idea that Polo exaggerated his own importance, writing that, "contrary to what has often been said ... Marco does not claim any very exalted position for himself in the Yuan empire."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw_2006_173_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-haw_2006_173-121">[120]</a></sup> He points out that Polo never claimed to hold high rank, such as a <i><a href="/wiki/Darughachi" title="Darughachi">darughachi</a></i>, who led a <i><a href="/wiki/Tumen_(unit)" title="Tumen (unit)">tumen</a></i> – a unit that was normally 10,000 strong. In fact, Polo does not even imply that he had led 1,000 personnel. Haw points out that Polo himself appears to state only that he had been an emissary of the <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">khan</a>, in a position with some esteem. According to Haw, this is a reasonable claim if Polo was, for example, a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keshig" title="Keshig">keshig</a></i> – a member of the imperial guard by the same name, which included as many as 14,000 individuals at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw_2006_173_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-haw_2006_173-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Haw explains how the earliest <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscripts</a> of Polo's accounts provide contradicting information about his role in Yangzhou, with some stating he was just a simple resident, others stating he was a governor, and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Ramusio" title="Giovanni Battista Ramusio">Ramusio's manuscript</a> claiming he was simply holding that office as a temporary substitute for someone else, yet all the manuscripts concur that he worked as an esteemed emissary for the khan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> Haw also objected to the approach to finding mention of Marco Polo in Chinese texts, contending that contemporaneous Europeans had little regard for using <a href="/wiki/Surname#history" title="Surname">surnames</a>, and a direct <a href="/wiki/Transcription_into_Chinese_characters" title="Transcription into Chinese characters">Chinese transcription</a> of the name "Marco" ignores the possibility of him taking on a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_name" title="Chinese name">Chinese</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_name" title="Mongolian name">Mongol name</a> that had no bearing or similarity with his <a href="/wiki/Christian_name" title="Christian name">Latin name</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haw_2006_173_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-haw_2006_173-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another controversial claim is at chapter 145 when the Book of Marvels states that the three Polos provided the Mongols with technical advice on building <a href="/wiki/Mangonel" title="Mangonel">mangonels</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Xiangyang" title="Battle of Xiangyang">Siege of Xiangyang</a>,
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Adonc distrent les .II. freres et lor filz meser Marc. "Grant Sire, nos avon avech nos en nostre mesnie homes qe firont tielz mangan qe giteront si grant pieres qe celes de la cité ne poront sofrir mes se renderont maintenant."</i></span>
</p><p><br/><span title="English-language text"><span lang="en">Then the two brothers and their son Marc said: "Great Lord, in our entourage we have men who will build such mangonels which launch such great stones, that the inhabitants of the city will not endure it and will immediately surrender."</span></span>
</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a class="external text" href="https://edizionicafoscari.unive.it/media/pdf/books/978-88-6969-224-6/978-88-6969-224-6_69TdyXc.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>Le devisement dou monde</i>, CXLV, ed. Mario Eusebi, p. 163</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Since the siege was over in 1273, before Marco Polo had arrived in China for the first time, the claim cannot be true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan,_D_page_223_114-3"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan,_D_page_223-114">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup>  The Mongol army that besieged Xiangyang did have foreign military engineers, but they were mentioned in Chinese sources as being from <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> and had Arabic names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-franke_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-franke-100">[99]</a></sup> In this respect, <a href="/wiki/Igor_de_Rachewiltz" title="Igor de Rachewiltz">Igor de Rachewiltz</a> recalls that the claim that the <i>three</i> Polo were present at the siege of Xiang-yang is not present in all manuscripts, but Niccolò and Matteo could have made this suggestion. Therefore, this claim seems a subsequent addition to give more credibility to the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Igor_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Igor-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Errors">Errors</span></h3>
<p>A number of errors in Marco Polo's account have been noted: for example, he described the bridge later known as <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_Bridge" title="Marco Polo Bridge">Marco Polo Bridge</a> as having twenty-four arches instead of eleven or thirteen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Igor_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-Igor-38">[38]</a></sup>  He also said that city wall of Khanbaliq had twelve gates when it had only eleven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> Archaeologists have also pointed out that Polo may have mixed up the details from the two <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">attempted invasions of Japan</a> by <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> in 1274 and 1281. Polo wrote of five-<a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">masted</a> ships, when archaeological excavations found that the ships, in fact, had only three masts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appropriation">Appropriation</span></h3>
<p>Wood accused Marco Polo of taking other people's accounts in his book, retelling other stories as his own, or basing his accounts on Persian guidebooks or other lost sources. For example, Sinologist <a href="/wiki/Francis_Woodman_Cleaves" title="Francis Woodman Cleaves">Francis Woodman Cleaves</a> noted that Polo's account of the voyage of the princess <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6k%C3%B6chin" title="Kököchin">Kököchin</a> from China to Persia to marry the Īl-khān in 1293 has been confirmed by a passage in the 15th-century Chinese work <i><a href="/wiki/Yongle_Encyclopedia" title="Yongle Encyclopedia">Yongle Encyclopedia</a></i> and by the Persian historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashid-al-Din_Hamadani" title="Rashid-al-Din Hamadani">Rashid-al-Din Hamadani</a> in his work <i><a href="/wiki/Jami%27_al-tawarikh" title="Jami' al-tawarikh">Jami' al-tawarikh</a></i>.  However, neither of these accounts mentions Polo or indeed any European as part of the bridal party,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cleaves_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-cleaves-98">[97]</a></sup> and Wood used the lack of mention of Polo in these works as an example of Polo's "retelling of a well-known tale".  Morgan, in Polo's defence, noted that even the princess herself was not mentioned in the Chinese source and that it would have been surprising if Polo had been mentioned by Rashid-al-Din.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan,_D_page_224_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan,_D_page_224-108">[107]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Igor_de_Rachewiltz" title="Igor de Rachewiltz">Igor de Rachewiltz</a> strongly criticised Wood's arguments in his review of her book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rachewiltz_1997_pp._34_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rachewiltz_1997_pp._34-127">[126]</a></sup> Rachewiltz argued that Marco Polo's account, in fact, allows the Persian and Chinese sources to be reconciled – by relaying the information that two of the three envoys sent (mentioned in the Chinese source and whose names accord with those given by Polo) had died during the voyage, it explains why only the third who survived, Coja/Khoja, was mentioned by Rashìd al-Dìn. Polo had therefore completed the story by providing information not found in either source. He also noted that the only Persian source that mentions the princess was not completed until 1310–11, therefore Marco Polo could not have learned the information from any Persian book.  According to de Rachewiltz, the concordance of Polo's detailed account of the princess with other independent sources that gave only incomplete information is proof of the veracity of Polo's story and his presence in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rachewiltz_1997_pp._34_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rachewiltz_1997_pp._34-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assessments">Assessments</span></h3>
<p>Morgan writes that since much of what <i>The Book of Marvels</i> has to say about China is "demonstrably correct",  any claim that Polo did not go to China "creates far more problems than it solves", therefore the "balance of probabilities" strongly suggests that Polo really did go to China, even if he exaggerated somewhat his importance in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> Haw dismisses the various anachronistic criticisms of Polo's accounts that started in the 17th century, and highlights Polo's accuracy in great part of his accounts, for example on features of the landscape such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_of_China" title="Grand Canal of China">Grand Canal of China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> "If Marco was a liar," Haw writes, "then he must have been an implausibly meticulous one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_T%C3%BCbingen" title="University of Tübingen">University of Tübingen</a> Sinologist and historian Hans Ulrich Vogel released a detailed analysis of Polo's description of currencies, <a href="/wiki/Salt_in_Chinese_history#Early_modern_technology" title="Salt in Chinese history">salt production</a> and revenues, and argued that the evidence supports his presence in China because he included details which he could not have otherwise known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vogel_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-vogel-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uot1204_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-uot1204-131">[130]</a></sup> Vogel noted that no other Western, Arab, or Persian sources have given such accurate and unique details about the currencies of China, for example, the shape and size of the paper, the use of seals, the various denominations of paper money as well as variations in currency usage in different regions of China, such as the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cowry_shell" title="Cowry shell">cowry shells</a> in Yunnan, details supported by archaeological evidence and Chinese sources compiled long after the Polos had left China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup>  His accounts of salt production and revenues from the salt monopoly are also accurate, and accord with Chinese documents of the Yuan era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> Economic historian <a href="/wiki/Mark_Elvin" title="Mark Elvin">Mark Elvin</a>, in his preface to Vogel's 2013 monograph, concludes that Vogel "demonstrates by specific example after specific example the ultimately overwhelming probability of the broad authenticity" of Polo's account. Many problems were caused by the oral transmission of the original text and the proliferation of significantly different hand-copied manuscripts. For instance, did Polo exert "political authority" (<i>seignora</i>) in Yangzhou or merely "sojourn" (<i>sejourna</i>) there? Elvin concludes that "those who doubted, although mistaken, were not always being casual or foolish", but "the case as a whole had now been closed": the book is, "in essence, authentic, and, when used with care, in broad terms to be trusted as a serious though obviously not always final, witness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_exploration">Further exploration</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">Europeans in Medieval China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_European_exploration_of_Asia" title="Chronology of European exploration of Asia">Chronology of European exploration of Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jorge_%C3%81lvares" title="Jorge Álvares">Jorge Álvares</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Perestrello" title="Rafael Perestrello">Rafael Perestrello</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="968" data-file-width="1334" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg/220px-ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg/330px-ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg/440px-ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ColombusNotesToMarcoPolo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Handwritten notes by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> on a Latin edition of Polo's book</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FraMauroDetailedMapInverted.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2245" data-file-width="2255" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/FraMauroDetailedMapInverted.jpg/220px-FraMauroDetailedMapInverted.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/FraMauroDetailedMapInverted.jpg/330px-FraMauroDetailedMapInverted.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/FraMauroDetailedMapInverted.jpg/440px-FraMauroDetailedMapInverted.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FraMauroDetailedMapInverted.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_map" title="Fra Mauro map">Fra Mauro map</a>, published <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1450</span> by the Venetian monk <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro" title="Fra Mauro">Fra Mauro</a></div></div></div>
<p>Other lesser-known European explorers had already travelled to China, such as <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_da_Pian_del_Carpine" title="Giovanni da Pian del Carpine">Giovanni da Pian del Carpine</a>, but Polo's book meant that his journey was the first to be widely known. <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> was inspired enough by Polo's description of the Far East to want to visit those lands for himself; a copy of the book was among his belongings, with handwritten annotations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landström_1967_27_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Landström_1967_27-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bento_de_G%C3%B3is" title="Bento de Góis">Bento de Góis</a>, inspired by Polo's writings of a Christian kingdom in the east, travelled 4,000 miles (6,400 km) in three years across Central Asia. He never found the kingdom but ended his travels at the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a> in 1605, proving that Cathay was what <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a> (1552–1610) called "China".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cartography">Cartography</span></h3><p>
Marco Polo's travels may have had some influence on the development of European <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a>, ultimately leading to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">European voyages of exploration</a> a century later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falchetta_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Falchetta-136">[135]</a></sup> The 1453 <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_map" title="Fra Mauro map">Fra Mauro map</a> was said by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Ramusio" title="Giovanni Battista Ramusio">Giovanni Battista Ramusio</a> (disputed by historian/cartographer Piero Falchetta, in whose work the quote appears) to have been partially based on the one brought from <a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a> by Marco Polo: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>That fine illuminated world map on parchment, which can still be seen in a large cabinet alongside the choir of their monastery [the Camaldolese monastery of San Michele di Murano] was by one of the brothers of the monastery, who took great delight in the study of cosmography, diligently drawn and copied from a most beautiful and very old nautical map and a world map that had been brought from Cathay by the most honourable Messer Marco Polo and his father.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Ramusio" title="Giovanni Battista Ramusio">Giovanni Battista Ramusio</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falchetta_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Falchetta-136">[135]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Though Marco Polo never produced a map that illustrated his journey, his family drew several maps to the Far East based on the traveller's accounts. These collections of maps were signed by Polo's three daughters, Fantina, Bellela and Moreta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History3_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-History3-137">[136]</a></sup> Not only did it contain maps of his journey, but also sea routes to Japan, Siberia's <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka_Peninsula" title="Kamchatka Peninsula">Kamchatka Peninsula</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a> and even to the coastlines of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, centuries before the rediscovery of the Americas by Europeans.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pasta_myth">Pasta myth</span></h3>
<p>There is a legend about Marco Polo importing <a href="/wiki/Pasta" title="Pasta">pasta</a> from China; however, it is actually a <a href="/wiki/List_of_common_misconceptions" title="List of common misconceptions">popular misconception</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> originating with the <i>Macaroni Journal</i>, published by a food industry association with the goal of promoting the use of pasta in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> Marco Polo describes in his book a food similar to "<a href="/wiki/Lasagna" title="Lasagna">lasagna</a>", but he uses a term with which he was already familiar. In fact, pasta had already been invented in Italy a long time before Marco Polo's travels to Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serventi_2002_10_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Serventi_2002_10-140">[139]</a></sup> According to the newsletter of the <a href="/wiki/National_Pasta_Association" title="National Pasta Association">National Macaroni Manufacturers Association</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serventi_2002_10_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Serventi_2002_10-140">[139]</a></sup> and food writer <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Steingarten" title="Jeffrey Steingarten">Jeffrey Steingarten</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steingarten_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steingarten-141">[140]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Durum_wheat" title="Durum wheat">durum wheat</a> was introduced by Arabs from Libya, <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Sicily" title="Emirate of Sicily">during their rule</a> over <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> in the late 9th century, thus predating Marco Polo's travels by about four centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steingarten_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Steingarten-141">[140]</a></sup> Steingarten also mentioned that <a href="/wiki/Jane_Grigson" title="Jane Grigson">Jane Grigson</a> believed the Marco Polo story to have originated in the 1920s or 30s in an advertisement for a Canadian spaghetti company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steingarten_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Steingarten-141">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lire_1000_(Marco_Polo).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1560" data-file-width="1413" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Lire_1000_%28Marco_Polo%29.jpg/220px-Lire_1000_%28Marco_Polo%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Lire_1000_%28Marco_Polo%29.jpg/330px-Lire_1000_%28Marco_Polo%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Lire_1000_%28Marco_Polo%29.jpg/440px-Lire_1000_%28Marco_Polo%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lire_1000_(Marco_Polo).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Italian banknote issued in 1982, portraying Marco Polo</div></div></div>
<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_sheep" title="Marco Polo sheep">Marco Polo sheep</a></b>, a subspecies of <i><a href="/wiki/Argali" title="Argali">Ovis ammon</a></i>, is named after the explorer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> who described it during his crossing of <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir</a> (ancient <a href="/wiki/Mount_Imeon" title="Mount Imeon">Mount Imeon</a>) in 1271.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[nb 2]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1851, a three-masted <a href="/wiki/Clipper" title="Clipper">clipper</a> built in Saint John, New Brunswick also took his name; the <b><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_(1851_ship)" title="Marco Polo (1851 ship)"><i>Marco Polo</i></a></b> was the first ship to sail around the world in under six months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>The airport in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> is named <b><a href="/wiki/Venice_Marco_Polo_Airport" title="Venice Marco Polo Airport">Venice Marco Polo Airport</a></b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Frequent-flyer_program" title="Frequent-flyer program">frequent flyer programme</a> of Hong Kong <a href="/wiki/Flag_carrier" title="Flag carrier">flag carrier</a> <a href="/wiki/Cathay_Pacific" title="Cathay Pacific">Cathay Pacific</a> is known as the "<b>Marco Polo Club</b>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State-owned" title="State-owned">state-owned</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shipping_company" title="Shipping company">shipping company</a>'s (<a href="/wiki/Jadrolinija" title="Jadrolinija">Jadrolinija</a>) ship connecting <a href="/wiki/Split,_Croatia" title="Split, Croatia">Split</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ancona" title="Ancona">Ancona</a> in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> is named after Marco Polo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jadrolinija_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jadrolinija-147">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Arts.2C_entertainment.2C_and_media"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts,_entertainment,_and_media">Arts, entertainment, and media</span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span></h5>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Adventures of Marco Polo">The Adventures of Marco Polo</a></i> (1938), directed by <a href="/wiki/Archie_Mayo" title="Archie Mayo">Archie Mayo</a></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_(1961_film)" title="Marco Polo (1961 film)">Marco Polo</a></i> (1961)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Marco_the_Magnificent" title="Marco the Magnificent">Marco the Magnificent</a></i> (1965)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Marco_(film)" title="Marco (film)">Marco</a></i> (1973), directed by <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Robbie" title="Seymour Robbie">Seymour Robbie</a></li>
<li><i>Marco Polo</i> (馬哥波羅) (1975), directed by <a href="/wiki/Chang_Cheh" title="Chang Cheh">Chang Cheh</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_Junior_Versus_the_Red_Dragon" title="Marco Polo Junior Versus the Red Dragon">Marco Polo Junior Versus the Red Dragon</a></i> (1972), Australian animated film by <a href="/wiki/Eric_Porter_(filmmaker)" title="Eric Porter (filmmaker)">Eric Porter</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Games">Games</span></h5>
<ul><li>The game "<a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_(game)" title="Marco Polo (game)">Marco Polo</a>" is a form of <a href="/wiki/Tag_(game)" title="Tag (game)">tag</a> played in a <a href="/wiki/Swimming_pool" title="Swimming pool">swimming pool</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-play_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-play-149">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup> or on land, with slightly modified rules.</li>
<li>Polo appears as a Great Explorer in the strategy video game <i><a href="/wiki/Civilization_Revolution" title="Civilization Revolution">Civilization Revolution</a></i> (2008).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>Marco Polo's 1292 voyage from <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> is used as a backdrop for the plot of <i><a href="/wiki/Uncharted_2:_Among_Thieves" title="Uncharted 2: Among Thieves">Uncharted 2: Among Thieves</a></i> (2009), where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nathan_Drake_(character)" title="Nathan Drake (character)">Nathan Drake</a> (the protagonist) searches for the <a href="/wiki/Cintamani" title="Cintamani">Cintamani</a> Stone, which was from the fabled city of <a href="/wiki/Shambhala" title="Shambhala">Shambhala</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup></li>
<li>A board game 'The Voyages of Marco Polo' plays over a map of Eurasia, with multiple routes to 'recreate' Polo's journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h5>
<p>The travels of Marco Polo are fictionalised in a number works, such as:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Brian_Oswald_Donn-Byrne" title="Brian Oswald Donn-Byrne">Brian Oswald Donn-Byrne</a>'s <i>Messer Marco Polo</i> (1921)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Italo_Calvino" title="Italo Calvino">Italo Calvino</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Invisible_Cities" title="Invisible Cities">Invisible Cities</a></i> (1972), in which Polo appears as a pivotal character.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gary_Jennings_(author)" title="Gary Jennings (author)">Gary Jennings</a>' novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Journeyer" title="The Journeyer">The Journeyer</a></i> (1984)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Avram_Davidson" title="Avram Davidson">Avram Davidson</a>'s novel (written with <a href="/wiki/Grania_Davis" title="Grania Davis">Grania Davis</a>) <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Marco_Polo_and_the_Sleeping_Beauty&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Marco Polo and the Sleeping Beauty (page does not exist)">Marco Polo and the Sleeping Beauty</a></i> (1988), a serio-comic fantasy with Polo as the protagonist.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Rollins" title="James Rollins">James Rollins</a>' <a href="/wiki/James_Rollins#SIGMA_Force_Book_4:_The_Judas_Strain_(2007)" title="James Rollins">SIGMA Force Book 4: <i>The Judas Strain</i></a> (2007), in which facts about Polo's travels and conjecture about secrets he kept are interleaved with modern-day action.</li></ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span></h5>
<ul><li>In the 1964 serial <i><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_(Doctor_Who)" title="Marco Polo (Doctor Who)">Marco Polo</a></i> of the television series <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">Doctor Who</a></i>, Polo was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Eden" title="Mark Eden">Mark Eden</a>.</li>
<li>The television miniseries, <i><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_(miniseries)" title="Marco Polo (miniseries)">Marco Polo</a></i> (1982), featuring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ken_Marshall" title="Ken Marshall">Ken Marshall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burt_Lancaster" title="Burt Lancaster">Burt Lancaster</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruocheng_Ying" title="Ruocheng Ying">Ruocheng Ying</a>, and directed by <a href="/wiki/Giuliano_Montaldo" title="Giuliano Montaldo">Giuliano Montaldo</a>, depicts Polo's travels. It won two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Awards</a>, and was nominated for six more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emmy_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emmy-155">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup></li>
<li>The television film, <i><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_(2007_film)" title="Marco Polo (2007 film)">Marco Polo</a></i> (2007), starring <a href="/wiki/Brian_Dennehy" title="Brian Dennehy">Brian Dennehy</a> as Kublai Khan, and <a href="/wiki/Ian_Somerhalder" title="Ian Somerhalder">Ian Somerhalder</a> as Marco, portrays Marco Polo being left alone in China while his uncle and father return to Venice, to be reunited with him many years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/In_the_Footsteps_of_Marco_Polo" title="In the Footsteps of Marco Polo">In the Footsteps of Marco Polo</a></i> (2009) is a <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> documentary about two friends (<a href="/wiki/Denis_Belliveau" title="Denis Belliveau">Denis Belliveau</a> and Francis O'Donnell) who conceived of the ultimate road trip to retrace Marco Polo's journey from Venice to China via land and sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/In_Search_of_Marco_Polo" title="In Search of Marco Polo">In Search of Marco Polo</a></i> (2013), a Croatian documentary miniseries written and directed by Miro Branković.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo_(TV_series)" title="Marco Polo (TV series)">Marco Polo</a></i> (2014–2016) is a <a href="/wiki/Netflix" title="Netflix">Netflix</a> television drama series about Marco Polo's early years in the court of <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> created by <a href="/wiki/John_Fusco" title="John Fusco">John Fusco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[158]</a></sup></li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Drog%C3%B6n_Ch%C3%B6gyal_Phagpa" title="Drogön Chögyal Phagpa">Drogön Chögyal Phagpa</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibetan</a> monk and confidant of Kublai Khan, mentions in his diaries that in 1271 a foreign friend of Kublai Khan visits—quite possibly one of the elder Polos or even Marco Polo himself, although, no name was given. If this is not the case, a more likely date for their arrival is 1275 (or 1274, according to the research of Japanese scholar Matsuo Otagi).(<a href="#CITEREFBritannica2002">Britannica 2002</a>, p. 571)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFYuleCordier1923">Yule &amp; Cordier 1923</a>, ch.18 states, "Then there are sheep here as big as asses; and their tails are so large and fat, that one tail shall weigh some 30 lb. They are fine fat beasts, and afford capital mutton."</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
